        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.7b13516"><meta property="og:title" content="Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/acsami.7b13516.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="In this article, carbon-coated Na3.64Fe2.18(P2O7)2 nanoparticles (∼10 nm) were successfully synthesized via a facile sol–gel method and employed as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. The results show that the carbon-coated Na3.64Fe2.18(P2O7)2 cathode delivers a high reversible capacity of 99 mAh g–1 at 0.2 C, outstanding cycling life retention of 96%, and high Coulomb efficiency of almost 100% even after 1000 cycles at 10 C. Furthermore, the electrochemical performances of full batteries consisting of carbon-coated Na3.64Fe2.18(P2O7)2 nanoparticles as the cathode and commercialized hard carbon as the anode are tested. The full batteries exhibit a reversible capacity of 86 mAh g–1 at 0.5 C and capacity retention of 80% after 100 cycles. Therefore, the above-mentioned cathode is a potential candidate for developing inexpensive sodium-ion batteries in large-scale energy storage with long life."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/acsami.7b13516.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8ecc9d9eb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8ecc9d9eb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '03918554-ed0f-4982-b1fa-6719da611a97'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8ecc9d9eb325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8ecc9d9eb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.7b13516" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="90da8ecc9d9eb325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Sodium-Rich Ferric Pyrophosphate Cathode for Stationary Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.7b13516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

China</span></div><div class="corresp-info"><strong>*</strong>E-mail: <a href="mailto:xumaowen@swu.edu.cn">xumaowen@swu.edu.cn</a> (M.X.).</div><div class="corresp-info"><strong>*</strong>E-mail: <a href="mailto:ecmli@swu.edu.cn">ecmli@swu.edu.cn</a> (C.L.).</div></div></div><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input class="pub-doi" type="hidden" name="pub-doi" value="10.1021/acsami.7b13516"><a href="/doi/epdf/10.1021/acsami.7b13516?ref=article_openPDF" title="PDF" target="_blank" class="article__btn__secondary" data-id="article_header_OpenPDF"><i class="icon-open-pdf"></i><span>Open PDF</span></a><a href="#_i8" title="Supporting Information" class="article__btn__secondary internalNav" data-id="article_header_SupportingInfo"><i class="icon-supporting-info"></i><span>Supporting Information (1)</span></a><a href="/servlet/linkout?suffix=s0&amp;dbid=16384&amp;doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;type=refOpenUrl&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fimp-primo.hosted.exlibrisgroup.com%2Fopenurl%2F44IMP%2FICL_VU1%3Fid%3Ddoi%3A10.1021%252Facsami.7b13516%26sid%3Dliteratum%253Aachs%26pmid%3D29231706%26genre%3Darticle%26aulast%3DShen%26date%3D2018%26atitle%3DSodium-Rich%2BFerric%2BPyrophosphate%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BStationary%2BRoom-Temperature%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26jtitle%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26title%3DACS%2BApplied%2BMaterials%2B%2526%2BInterfaces%26volume%3D10%26issue%3D1%26spage%3D502%26epage%3D508%26issn%3D1944-8244" title="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON" class="issue-item_open-url sfxLink"><img src="/userimages/9157ccea-6c85-4e5f-ac2a-6a9fd9422c17/sfxbutton-180e067c810" alt="OpenURL IMPERIAL COLLEGE LONDON"></a></div></div></div><div class="article_header-meta"><div class="article_header-meta-left"><input type="hidden" name="journalCodeForjhpLink" value="aamick"><input type="hidden" name="journalNameForjhpLink" value="ACS Applied Materials &amp; Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">In this article, carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (∼10 nm) were successfully synthesized via a facile sol–gel method and employed as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries. The results show that the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> cathode delivers a high reversible capacity of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C, outstanding cycling life retention of 96%, and high Coulomb efficiency of almost 100% even after 1000 cycles at 10 C. Furthermore, the electrochemical performances of full batteries consisting of carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles as the cathode and commercialized hard carbon as the anode are tested. The full batteries exhibit a reversible capacity of 86 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 C and capacity retention of 80% after 100 cycles. Therefore, the above-mentioned cathode is a potential candidate for developing inexpensive sodium-ion batteries in large-scale energy storage with long life.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D502%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DBolei%2BShen%252C%2BMaowen%2BXu%252C%2BYubin%2BNiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D1%26contentID%3Dacsami.7b13516%26title%3DSodium-Rich%2BFerric%2BPyrophosphate%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BStationary%2BRoom-Temperature%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D508%26publicationDate%3DJanuary%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">

                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na3.64fe2.182&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na3.64Fe2.18%28P2O7%292">Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Long-cycle&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_long-cycle">long-cycle</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Full-cell&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_full-cell">full-cell</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Demand for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) is increasing with the rapid development of portable electronic devices and the popularity of electric vehicles. However, further application of LIBs is restricted by several shortcomings, such as the high price and rare resources of lithium. In comparison, sodium is much more abundant than lithium, which means that the cost of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is lower. Therefore, SIBs are undoubtedly a good alternative to lithium-ion batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p">In recent years, research on SIB cathodes has tended to be more developed. There are usually two main types of SIB cathodes: one is of layered transition-metal oxides and the other is of polyanion materials. Layered transition-metal oxides, such as Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>CoO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>VO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> Na<sub>2/3</sub>Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> and Na<sub>0.5</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mn<sub>0.75</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> have a high theoretical capacity and various voltage platforms. However, the cycle stability of these materials is unsatisfactory. Examples of polyanionic materials are Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>VOP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> Na<sub>7</sub>V<sub>4</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>4</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10, 11)</a> Na<sub>4</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> NaFePO<sub>4</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 14 to 16">(14-16)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>FeP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17-19)</a> Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>MnP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22, 23)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>CoP<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 25 to 28">(25-28)</a> and so on. Different from layered metal oxides, these polyanion materials have more stable crystal structures, which lead to long, stable cycles, enhanced safety, and adjustable voltages. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Furthermore, the voltages of these materials are often higher than those of layered transition-metal oxides because of the inductive effect from phosphorus. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Thus, polyanion materials may be more promising for commercialization of SIBs. Among these polyanion materials, the newly reported ferric pyrophosphate sodium host series Na<sub>4−α</sub>M<sub>2+α/2</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> has gained much attention and some nonstoichiometric compounds, such as Na<sub>3.12</sub>Fe<sub>2.44</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30, 31)</a> and Na<sub>3.32</sub>Fe<sub>2.34</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> have been reported. In these ferric pyrophosphate family members, sodium-rich cathode may promote a better cycle stability. Hence, we try to design and synthesize a type of high-sodium-ratio compound of ferric pyrophosphate.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, a new sodium-rich Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> compound as a cathode material for SIBs is explored for the first time. Considering that polyanion materials suffer from poor conductivity and atmosphere sensitivity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> nanosized and carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was designed and synthesized by a simple sol–gel method. The as-prepared Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> shows excellent electrochemical performances.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">All chemical reagents were of analytical grade and used without further purification. Sol–gel and preoxidation methods were combined to achieve both carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. First, 1.7614 g of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved into 30 mL of distilled water to obtain a brown solution, which was followed by a slow addition of 0.3858 g of vitamin C. Then, 2.2362 g of citric acid, as a chelating agent, 0.3858 g of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and 0.9202 g of NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> were added and dissolved in the solution. Continuous stirring was performed until a clear solution was obtained. The solution was heated to 80 °C with continuous stirring for 8 h and then dried at 120 °C under vacuum to obtain a dry gel. To obtain the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, the dry gel was heated in a H<sub>2</sub>/Ar (10:90) atmosphere at 600 °C for 24 h. The color of the as-synthesized sample was black (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p last">The pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> particles were synthesized by the preoxidation method. The dry gel was first heated in air at 600 °C for 8 h to eliminate organic species and carbon. After annealing, the sample was heated in a H<sub>2</sub>/Ar (10:90) atmosphere at 600 °C for 48 h; the color of the as-obtained sample was deep green (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD, MAXima-X XRD-7000) using Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5416 Å) was used to analyze the crystalline structure of the material. The test was performed in step mode with a fixed time of 12 s and a step size of 0.02°. An inductively coupled plasma (ICP) optical emission spectrometer (Optima 8000) was employed to measure the content of Na and Fe. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Q50) in flowing nitrogen and air was used to monitor the reaction process and measure the carbon content of the sample. The specific surface area of the samples was analyzed by a Quadrasorb evo 2QDS-MP-30 surface area analyzer (Quantachrome Instruments). The morphologies and structures of the samples were observed by field-emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM; JEOL-6300F) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM; JEOL-2100). The chemical element distribution was determined by energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS; JEOL-6300F). Finally, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to analyze the elemental composition at different states using a Thermo Scientific ESCALAB 250Xi electron spectrometer.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode consisted of as-synthesized cathode materials, conductor acetylene black, and binder poly(vinylidene difluoride) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 with <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone as the solvent. The mixed slurry was evenly spread on pristine Al foil and dried in vacuum for 12 h at 120 °C. The resulting mass loading of the working electrode is 1.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. A sodium foil was employed as the reference and counter electrode. Celgard 2400 was employed as a separator to separate the working electrode and counter electrode, and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and diethyl carbonate with 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate as an additive was used as the electrolyte. The as-prepared electrode, separator, and electrolyte were fabricated in CR2032-type coin cells in a high-purity Ar-filled glovebox with both the moisture and oxygen contents below 1 ppm. A LAND cycler (Wuhan LAND Electronics Co., Ltd., China) was used to test the galvanostatic discharge and charge cycling of the batteries at different rates. The electrochemical capacity measurements were performed in the voltage range of 1.6–4.0 V. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were carried out by an Arbin instruments testing system at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles were formed via the facile sol–gel method. As schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, all chemical reagents were initially distributed evenly in the precursor. The decomposition of the organic species led to the formation of the porous structure of the nanoparticles, and the extended annealing process prompted crystal growth. Because the chemical reagents were evenly distributed in the framework of porous carbon, the growth of crystal was limited and the crystal could only grow along a plane, leading to the formation of the nanoparticle product. Regarding pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, the nanoparticles will agglomerate to form large bulks in the absence of porous carbon. The porous structure of materials can allow the electrolyte to easily penetrate every corner, which is an advantage for Na<sup>+</sup> and electron migration. Meanwhile, the well-coated carbon can protect the crystal structure of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> during Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation, which supports the stability of the cycle.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration for the formation of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a displays the whole pattern fitting (WPF) refinement X-ray diffraction (XRD) profile of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> product, and the XRD pattern of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> without the carbon coat is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. The WPF refinement was carried out and achieved results with <i>a</i> = 6.39641 Å, <i>b</i> = 9.41633 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.93129 Å, α = 64.935°, β = 80.186°, γ = 73.104°, and <i>V</i> = 569.8 Å<sup>3</sup>. All peaks agree well with those of isostructural Na<sub>3.64</sub>M<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (M = Ni and Mg), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> and no extra peaks from impurities were observed, demonstrating the high purity of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. The inset displays the crystal structure of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, which reveals that it contains PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra and FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. FeO<sub>6</sub> and PO<sub>4</sub> are linked together to form a three-dimensional network structure, and the thus-formed large tunnel is conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> mobility. It is worth noting that the crystal structures of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>3.12</sub>Fe<sub>2.44</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> are very similar, but there are also some differences (the crystal structures comparison of these materials is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The Fe<sub>3</sub> atoms in Na<sub>3.12</sub>Fe<sub>2.44</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> were replaced by two Na atoms (Na<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>5</sub>) in Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. In addition, in the Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> crystal, there are more sodium atoms instead of iron atoms (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). It is known that the Na ions will overextract at high voltage, which leads to structural degradation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The extra Na ions can alleviate this phenomenon. Furthermore, during the charging process, more Na ions can stay in the crystal structure. The extra Na ions can stabilize the crystal structure, which leads to better cycle performance. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b shows the wide range scanning spectrum of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C, and it can be seen from the results that the sample is composed of Na, Fe, P, O, and C elements. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c displays the XPS narrow spectra of Fe 2p, and it is obvious that the Fe 2p signal consists of two components due to spin–orbit coupling (Fe <sup>2</sup>p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe <sup>2</sup>p<sub>1/2</sub>), which can confirm the +2 valence state of Fe in this sample, suggesting that the sample consists of only one valence state of iron atoms. Furthermore, the <sup>2</sup>p<sub>3/2</sub> peak shows a distinct satellite around 719.0 eV. This satellite is caused by a dynamic charge transfer during the photoemission process (these charge-transfer satellites appear in the spectra of most transition-metal compounds). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35-37)</a></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0001.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) PXRD pattern of the as-prepared product. The inset represents its crystal structure. (b) XPS wide range scanning spectra of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C. (c) XPS narrow spectra of Fe 2p.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further verify the stoichiometric ratio of the material, ICP spectrometry was conducted. The result is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>; the concentrations of Na and Fe are 0.526 and 0.765 mg L<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The theoretical mass ratio of Na to Fe is 0.6873, and the measured mass ratio of Na to Fe is 0.6876. The results indicate that high-purity Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was successfully synthesized.</div><div class="NLM_p">As observed from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the surface of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C is very rough and different sizes of pores are randomly connected with each other to form the porous framework, which is beneficial for electrolyte penetration and Na<sup>+</sup> migration. By comparison, pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> consists of large size and no porous structure (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). In the low-resolution TEM image (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b), it can be seen that about 10 nm nanoparticles are uniformly dispersed in the carbon framework. The high-resolution TEM image is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. The interplanar distance of 0.307 nm corresponds well to the (200) lattice plane. The selected area (electron) diffraction (SAED) pattern is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. To obtain more detailed surface information of the two samples, Brunauer–Emmett–Teller measurements were made and are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. According to the results, the surface area and pore volume of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C are 74 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 0.119 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, the pore diameters are concentrated to approximately 2 nm. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> shows the isotherms of pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, which has a surface area of only 1.89 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. TGA was used to analyze the carbon content of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C, and the result is displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d. After heating in air, a white product was obtained. According to the graphics in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, the weight of the as-prepared product remained ∼89% after heating, which means that the as-prepared Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C has a carbon content of ∼11%. The formation process of the as-prepared products based on TGA is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. According to the results, the sample formation temperature was around 460 °C. To obtain the best crystallinity and make sure that the residual organic species were completely decomposed, 600 °C heating temperature for 24 h was maintained. Furthermore, EDS element mapping analysis was employed to identify the element distribution of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>; all elements are found to be homogeneously distributed throughout the selected Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> plates from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0002.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) FESEM and (b) TEM images of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. (c) Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C. The inset shows the pore size distribution of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C. (d) Carbon content of the as-prepared product basis on thermogravimetric analysis (TGA).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The theoretical capacity of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> is 106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>; 2.18 Na ion is reversibly de-/intercalated by the oxidation and reduction of 2.18 Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup>. The electrochemical behaviors of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C were further studied by CV at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). Four pairs of redox peaks were observed (2.5/2.45, 2.98/2.89, 3.24/3.20, and 3.42/3.32 V) with equilibrium potentials of 2.475, 2.935, 3.22, and 3.37 V, respectively, corresponding to the phase transition during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction reactions. The total potentials were around 3 V, which correspond well with the galvanostatic charge–discharge profile in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b. In <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b, during the first charge cycle, the voltage increased to 4 V from open circuit voltage, resulting in a capacity of 108.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity of the initial cycle was a little higher than the theoretical capacity of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> (106 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). This phenomenon may be due to the slight oxidization of Na<sub>3.64</sub>F<sub>e2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C, which forms a layer of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> on the surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The tiny amount of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> will react with the electrolyte, which leads to irreversible capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> The peak at 3.12 V in the CV test may correspond to the reaction. Then, the voltage decreased to 1.6 V, resulting in a capacity of 99 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Two obvious discharge platforms can also be observed at 3 and 2.4 V, which correspond well with the equilibrium potentials mentioned in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. Three pairs of redox peaks (2.98/2.89, 3.24/3.20, and 3.42/3.32 V) were combined to produce a 3 V equilibrium potential, and the redox peak at 2.5/2.45 V corresponds with an equilibrium potential of 2.4 V.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0003.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. (a) CV at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at 0.2 C. (c) Rate capability. (d) Discharge curves at 0.2–10 C. (e) Long cycling performance. (f) Comparison with other works.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To characterize the recovery performance of the material, various current densities (0.2, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C) were employed to evaluate the rate capability; the results are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c. The charge–discharge curves at different rates are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d and suggest that even under the drastic changes in current the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles display a good recovery performance. Importantly, at 10 C, the material exhibits excellent stability. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e displays the long cycling performance of the carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles, which shows discharge specific capacities of 101 and 88 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current rates were 0.2 and 1 C, respectively. After 900 cycles, the discharge capacity was 80 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a capacity retention of 90.9%. Furthermore, when the current rate was 2 C, the material exhibited a discharge specific capacity of 66.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which increased to 74 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 1000 cycles, the discharge capacity remained 66 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, revealing good stability of the material. Most importantly, when the current rate reached 10 C, the discharge capacity remained 40 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a high capacity retention of 96% after 1000 cycles. The excellent properties of the material can be attributed to the porous structure and the unique nanosize of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, which increase the conductivity and the diffusion coefficient of the Na ion. Furthermore, the cycle performance of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C was significantly better than that of other ferric pyrophosphate family members (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f). The electrochemical performances of pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. As can be seen from <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>, the electrochemical properties of pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> are not good. The discharge capacity achieves only 61 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 C. In addition, according to the CV, the polarization of pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> is very serious. This phenomenon could be attributed to the poor conductivity and the large size of pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. However, pristine Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> has very good cycling performance, which may be attributed to the fact that the extra Na ions can stabilize the crystal structure.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further demonstrate the application prospects, a coin-type sodium-ion full battery and a soft-package sodium-ion full battery with Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C as the cathode and presodiated hard carbon as the anode were assembled (the capacity ratio of cathode to anode is 1:1.25). The electrochemical performances of the hard carbon are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a illustrates the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at 0.5 C. A voltage platform of 2.9 V and a capacity of 86 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be observed. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b displays the cycling performance of the full battery at 0.5 C. More than 80% capacity remains after 100 cycles. The rate performance shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c proves that the as-prepared full battery can be stably cycled at various rates from 0.2 to 5 C. The discharge capacity varies from 85.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C to 65.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 C. Even at 2 C, the discharge capacity remains at 45.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, nearly 53% of the capacity at 0.2 C. The corresponding discharge curves are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d. Furthermore, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/suppl_file/am7b13516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> shows a commercial red light-emitting diode (LED) powered by the assembled soft-package full batteries, further indicating the application prospects of the Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>/C//hard carbon system.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/medium/am-2017-13516q_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of the Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub>//hard carbon full batteries. (a) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles at 0.5 C. (b) Long cycling performance at 0.5 C. (c) Rate capability of full batteries. (d) Discharge curves at 0.2–5 C. (The capacity ratio of cathode to anode is 1:1.25.)</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.7b13516/asset/images/large/am-2017-13516q_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.7b13516&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, carbon-coated Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are fabricated to investigate sodium de-/intercalation behavior for the first time. We found that the as-prepared sodium-rich Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> exhibits outstanding cycle capability. Such excellent cycle stability is attributed to the supporting effect of extra sodium ions. The cycle performance of Na<sub>3.64</sub>Fe<sub>2.18</sub>(P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>)<sub>2</sub> is obviously better in the ferric pyrophosphate family members. Meanwhile, the unique porous network structure provides interconnected open pores and effective carbon coating, which allows electrolyte penetration and improves the electronic conductivity of uniform nanoparticles. Because of the low cost, simple synthesis method, nontoxic nature, and excellent electrochemical performances of the as-prepared product, we believe that this material is very promising for future applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.7b13516" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.7b13516</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Concentration and mass ratio of Na and Fe based on ICP;                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical investigation of the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Berthelot, R.; Carlier, D.; Delmas, C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">74-80</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrk1JcT1P9myrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljuHDlkoE62mw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXhsFKisrvJ&amp;md5=d9d06332c38e3009393054b672c767f7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600275"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ni, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv. Sci.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">4</span><span class="NLM_elocation-id">1600275</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/advs.201600275</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D4%26publication_year%3D2017%26journal%3DAdv.%2BSci.%26author%3DQ.%2BNi%26author%3DY.%2BBai%26author%3DF.%2BWu%26author%3DC.%2BWu%26title%3DPolyanion-Type%2BElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadvs.201600275&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.7b13516&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadvs.201600275&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni Qiao; Bai Ying; Wu Feng; Wu Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1600275</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRzOrk04pyNfsjl5L8hwNX0fW6udTcc2eZnZzPJx2TVDrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1cvgtVOitA%253D%253D&amp;md5=f4bc597b44df5e4e4c0b0a698c9a4d56</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1149/1.3555102"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Didier, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Guignard, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Denage, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Szajwaj, O.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ito, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Saadoune, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Darriet, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Delmas, C.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na-Deintercalation from NaVO<sub>2</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Electrochem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This paper describes the prepn. of NaxVO2 phases by electrochem. deintercalation.  The curves V = f(x) show that the process is reversible up to x = 0.5 and they indicate multiple single phase domains in the range 1 &lt; x &lt; 0.5.  Two single phases were successfully isolated for Na1/2VO2 and Na2/3VO2 compns.; the authors report here the 1st x-ray powder diffraction measurements of these phases.  They present a O'3-type monoclinic distortion of the O3 rhombohedral phase obsd. for Na1VO2.  The evolution of cell parameters with Na deintercalation is comparable with other AxMO2 layered oxides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoYtKHyy3LVlrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgJPtlvXS9gCg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXivVGisrw%253D&amp;md5=f443243e7ccaf4e42e86f5a389bd437f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/S0167-2738(99)00145-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Blesa, M.-C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Moran, E.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Leo’n, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Santamaria, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tornero, J.-D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mene’ndez, N.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title"><i>a</i>-NaFeO: ionic conductivity and sodium extraction</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Solid State Ionics</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1999</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">126</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">81</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">87</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
